## Chapter 1:9: Energy Modeling:




### Introduction

Water heating systems are an essential component of modern buildings, providing hot water for various purposes such as cooking, cleaning, and personal hygiene. The design and operation of these systems have a significant impact on energy consumption and overall building performance. In this chapter, we will explore the fundamentals of water heating systems, including their types, components, and energy considerations.

We will begin by discussing the different types of water heating systems, including traditional storage water heaters, instantaneous water heaters, and heat pump water heaters. Each type has its own advantages and disadvantages, and understanding these differences is crucial for selecting the most suitable system for a particular building.

Next, we will delve into the components of water heating systems, including the heat source, storage tank, and control mechanisms. We will also discuss the role of each component in the overall operation of the system and how they interact with each other.

Finally, we will examine the energy considerations of water heating systems. This includes the energy inputs required for heating water, as well as the energy losses that occur during the heating process. We will also discuss strategies for optimizing energy use and reducing energy consumption in water heating systems.

By the end of this chapter, readers will have a comprehensive understanding of water heating systems and their role in building energy performance. This knowledge will be valuable for architects, engineers, and building owners in designing and operating efficient and sustainable water heating systems.




### Subsection: 19.1a Storage Water Heaters

Storage water heaters, also known as tank-type water heaters, are a common type of water heater used in household and commercial settings. These devices are designed to continuously keep water hot and ready for use, making them ideal for applications that require high flow rates for limited periods.

#### Design and Operation

Storage water heaters consist of a cylindrical vessel or container that is designed to withstand hydrostatic pressure. The vessel is connected to the incoming mains supply, and a pressure reducing valve may be employed to limit the pressure to a safe level for the vessel. The vessel may also incorporate an electrical resistance heater, a heat pump, or a gas or oil burner that heats water directly.

The operation of storage water heaters is relatively simple. Cold water is fed into the vessel, and the heat source, such as an electrical resistance heater, heat pump, or gas or oil burner, is used to heat the water. The heated water is then stored in the vessel until it is needed. When hot water is needed, it is drawn off from the vessel, and the vessel is refilled with cold water, which is then heated again.

#### Types of Storage Water Heaters

There are several types of storage water heaters, each with its own advantages and disadvantages. The most common types include:

- **Electric Storage Water Heaters:** These water heaters use electricity as their heat source and are typically less expensive than other types of water heaters. However, they can be expensive to operate, especially in areas with high electricity rates.

- **Gas Storage Water Heaters:** These water heaters use natural gas or propane as their heat source and are typically less expensive to operate than electric water heaters. However, they require a gas line connection, which may not be available in all locations.

- **Oil Storage Water Heaters:** These water heaters use heating oil as their heat source and are typically less expensive to operate than electric or gas water heaters. However, they require a fuel oil tank, which can be expensive to install and maintain.

- **Solar Storage Water Heaters:** These water heaters use solar energy as their heat source and are typically the most expensive to install. However, they can be the most cost-effective to operate in the long run, especially in areas with high electricity rates.

#### Energy Considerations

The energy considerations for storage water heaters are similar to those for other types of water heaters. The main energy inputs are the heat source and the energy losses that occur during the heating process. The energy losses can be minimized by insulating the vessel and the pipes connecting the vessel to the incoming mains supply.

In addition to the energy inputs and losses, there are also energy considerations related to the size and capacity of the vessel. Larger vessels require more energy to heat and maintain, but they can also provide a higher flow rate of hot water. Therefore, the size and capacity of the vessel should be carefully considered based on the specific needs and requirements of the building.

#### Conclusion

Storage water heaters are a common type of water heater used in household and commercial settings. They are designed to continuously keep water hot and ready for use, making them ideal for applications that require high flow rates for limited periods. By understanding the design, operation, and energy considerations of storage water heaters, building professionals can make informed decisions about the most suitable type of water heater for a particular building.





### Subsection: 19.1b Tankless Water Heaters

Tankless water heaters, also known as instantaneous water heaters, are a type of water heater that heats water only when it is needed. Unlike storage water heaters, which store and continuously heat a reservoir of water, tankless water heaters heat water on demand. This makes them a popular choice for applications that require a high flow rate for a limited period, such as in commercial settings.

#### Design and Operation

Tankless water heaters consist of a heat exchanger that is designed to heat water as it passes through. The heat exchanger is typically made of copper or stainless steel, and it is designed to withstand high temperatures and pressures. The heat exchanger is connected to the incoming mains supply, and a flow sensor is used to detect when water is flowing through the system.

When hot water is needed, the flow sensor detects the flow of water and activates the heat exchanger. The heat exchanger then heats the water as it passes through, and the heated water is delivered to the point of use. The heat exchanger remains active until the flow of water stops, at which point it shuts off.

#### Types of Tankless Water Heaters

There are several types of tankless water heaters, each with its own advantages and disadvantages. The most common types include:

- **Electric Tankless Water Heaters:** These water heaters use electricity as their heat source and are typically less expensive than other types of tankless water heaters. However, they can be expensive to operate, especially in areas with high electricity rates.

- **Gas Tankless Water Heaters:** These water heaters use natural gas or propane as their heat source and are typically less expensive to operate than electric tankless water heaters. However, they require a gas line connection, which may not be available in all locations.

- **Solar Tankless Water Heaters:** These water heaters use solar energy as their heat source and are typically the most expensive type of tankless water heater. However, they can be the most cost-effective in the long run, especially in areas with high solar irradiance.

- **Heat Pump Tankless Water Heaters:** These water heaters use a heat pump to transfer heat from the ambient air or ground to the water, making them a more energy-efficient option than other types of tankless water heaters. However, they require a larger installation space and can be expensive to install.

#### Advantages and Disadvantages

Tankless water heaters offer several advantages over storage water heaters. These include:

- **Endless Hot Water:** Tankless water heaters provide hot water on demand, so there is no limit to the amount of hot water that can be used.

- **Space Savings:** Tankless water heaters are typically smaller than storage water heaters, making them ideal for applications where space is limited.

- **Energy Efficiency:** Tankless water heaters only heat water when it is needed, so they are more energy-efficient than storage water heaters.

However, tankless water heaters also have some disadvantages, including:

- **Initial Cost:** Tankless water heaters can be more expensive to purchase and install than storage water heaters.

- **Maintenance:** Tankless water heaters require regular maintenance to ensure optimal performance.

- **Flow Rate Limitations:** Tankless water heaters may not be able to provide a high enough flow rate for applications that require a high flow rate for a prolonged period.

In conclusion, tankless water heaters are a popular choice for applications that require a high flow rate for a limited period. While they offer several advantages, they also have some limitations that must be considered. As with any type of water heater, it is important to carefully consider the specific needs and requirements of the application when choosing a water heater.





### Subsection: 19.1c Heat Pump Water Heaters

Heat pump water heaters are a type of water heater that uses a heat pump to transfer heat from the surrounding air or ground to the water. This makes them a more energy-efficient alternative to traditional water heaters, as they use a renewable energy source to heat the water.

#### Design and Operation

Heat pump water heaters consist of a heat pump, a storage tank, and a heat exchanger. The heat pump is connected to the storage tank, which holds the water to be heated. The heat pump is designed to transfer heat from the surrounding air or ground to the water in the tank. The heat exchanger is used to transfer the heat from the water to the point of use.

The operation of a heat pump water heater is similar to that of a refrigerator. The heat pump contains a refrigerant that is circulated between two coils, one of which is in the storage tank and the other of which is in the surrounding air or ground. The refrigerant absorbs heat from the air or ground and transfers it to the water in the tank. The heated water is then stored in the tank until it is needed.

#### Types of Heat Pump Water Heaters

There are several types of heat pump water heaters, each with its own advantages and disadvantages. The most common types include:

- **Air Source Heat Pump Water Heaters:** These water heaters use the surrounding air as their heat source and are typically less expensive than ground source heat pump water heaters. However, they may not be as efficient in colder climates.

- **Ground Source Heat Pump Water Heaters:** These water heaters use the ground as their heat source and are typically more efficient than air source heat pump water heaters. However, they require a larger initial investment and may not be suitable for all locations.

- **Hybrid Heat Pump Water Heaters:** These water heaters combine a heat pump with a traditional water heater, providing the benefits of both. They are typically more expensive than traditional water heaters, but can provide significant energy savings.

### Conclusion

Heat pump water heaters are a promising technology for reducing energy consumption in buildings. By using a renewable energy source to heat water, they can provide significant energy savings compared to traditional water heaters. However, their initial cost and suitability for all locations may limit their widespread adoption. As technology continues to advance, heat pump water heaters are likely to become an increasingly important component of sustainable building design.





### Subsection: 19.2a Energy Factor

The energy factor (EF) is a measure of the energy efficiency of a water heater. It is defined as the ratio of the heat output of the water heater to the total energy input. The energy factor is typically expressed as a decimal or a percentage.

#### Calculating the Energy Factor

The energy factor of a water heater can be calculated using the following formula:

$$
EF = \frac{Heat\ Output}{Total\ Energy\ Input}
$$

The heat output is the amount of heat transferred from the water heater to the water. The total energy input includes all forms of energy used to operate the water heater, including electricity, gas, or oil.

#### Energy Factor of Different Types of Water Heaters

The energy factor of different types of water heaters can vary significantly. Traditional storage water heaters typically have an energy factor of around 0.55, while heat pump water heaters can have an energy factor of up to 0.95. This is due to the fact that heat pump water heaters use a renewable energy source to heat the water, making them more energy-efficient.

#### Importance of Energy Factor

The energy factor is an important consideration when choosing a water heater. A water heater with a higher energy factor will be more energy-efficient and will result in lower energy costs over time. Additionally, in some regions, the energy factor is used to determine the energy efficiency standards for water heaters, making it a crucial factor in the design and operation of water heating systems.

#### Energy Factor and Energy Efficiency

The energy factor is closely related to the energy efficiency of a water heater. As the energy factor increases, the energy efficiency of the water heater also increases. This means that a water heater with a higher energy factor will use less energy to heat the water, resulting in lower energy costs and a more sustainable operation.

#### Factors Affecting Energy Factor

The energy factor of a water heater can be affected by various factors, including the type of water heater, the design of the water heater, and the operating conditions. For example, a heat pump water heater will have a higher energy factor than a traditional storage water heater due to its use of a renewable energy source. Additionally, the design of the water heater, such as the size and insulation, can also affect the energy factor. Lastly, the operating conditions, such as the temperature and flow rate of the water, can also impact the energy factor.

#### Conclusion

The energy factor is a crucial factor in the design and operation of water heating systems. It is a measure of the energy efficiency of a water heater and is affected by various factors. By understanding the energy factor and its importance, we can make informed decisions when choosing and operating water heaters, leading to more energy-efficient and sustainable water heating systems.





### Subsection: 19.2b Energy Star Water Heaters

Energy Star water heaters are a type of water heater that meets the energy efficiency standards set by the U.S. Environmental Protection Agency (EPA). These standards are designed to reduce energy consumption and promote the use of renewable energy sources. Energy Star water heaters are becoming increasingly popular due to their energy efficiency and potential for cost savings.

#### Energy Star Standards for Water Heaters

The Energy Star program sets minimum energy efficiency standards for water heaters based on their size and type. These standards are constantly updated to reflect advancements in technology and energy efficiency. For example, the current standards for gas and oil-fired water heaters require an energy factor of at least 0.62, while the standards for electric water heaters require an energy factor of at least 0.90.

#### Types of Energy Star Water Heaters

There are several types of Energy Star water heaters available, including traditional storage water heaters, heat pump water heaters, and tankless water heaters. Each type has its own unique energy factor and potential for energy savings.

Traditional storage water heaters, also known as tank-style water heaters, are the most common type of water heater. They use a storage tank to store hot water and have an energy factor of around 0.55.

Heat pump water heaters, also known as hybrid water heaters, use a heat pump to transfer heat from the air or ground to the water. They have an energy factor of up to 0.95 and can be more energy-efficient than traditional storage water heaters.

Tankless water heaters, also known as on-demand water heaters, heat water on demand and do not require a storage tank. They have an energy factor of around 0.82 and can be more energy-efficient than traditional storage water heaters.

#### Benefits of Energy Star Water Heaters

Energy Star water heaters offer several benefits, including potential for energy savings, reduced greenhouse gas emissions, and promotion of renewable energy sources. By meeting the Energy Star standards, these water heaters can help reduce energy consumption and promote the use of more efficient and sustainable technologies.

#### Factors Affecting Energy Star Water Heaters

The energy efficiency of Energy Star water heaters can be affected by various factors, including the type of energy source used, the size of the water heater, and the climate in which it is used. For example, in colder climates, heat pump water heaters may be more energy-efficient than traditional storage water heaters due to their ability to transfer heat from the air or ground.

#### Conclusion

Energy Star water heaters are an important step towards reducing energy consumption and promoting the use of renewable energy sources. By meeting the Energy Star standards, these water heaters can help reduce energy costs and promote a more sustainable future. As technology continues to advance, it is important for water heaters to meet these standards to ensure energy efficiency and promote a more sustainable future.





### Subsection: 19.2c Water Heater Insulation

Water heater insulation is an important aspect of energy efficiency in water heating systems. It involves the use of insulation materials to reduce heat loss and maintain the temperature of the water. This not only helps in conserving energy but also reduces the cost of heating water.

#### Types of Water Heater Insulation

There are two main types of water heater insulation: tank insulation and pipe insulation. Tank insulation involves insulating the storage tank of the water heater, while pipe insulation involves insulating the pipes that carry hot water from the water heater to other parts of the building.

#### Benefits of Water Heater Insulation

Insulating water heaters can provide significant energy savings. According to the U.S. Department of Energy, insulating a water heater tank can reduce heat loss by up to 45%, resulting in cost savings of up to 9% on water heating bills. Additionally, insulating the pipes can reduce heat loss during distribution, further improving energy efficiency.

#### Materials Used for Water Heater Insulation

The most commonly used materials for water heater insulation are fiberglass, foam, and reflective insulation. Fiberglass insulation is the most popular and is available in pre-cut jackets for water heaters. It is also relatively inexpensive. Foam insulation, on the other hand, is more expensive but provides better insulation. Reflective insulation, also known as radiant barrier insulation, is made of aluminum foil and is used to reflect heat back into the water heater.

#### Installation of Water Heater Insulation

Installing water heater insulation is a relatively simple process. For tank insulation, the insulation jacket is placed over the tank, ensuring that it fits snugly. For pipe insulation, the insulation is wrapped around the pipes, making sure to cover all exposed areas. It is important to note that the insulation should not touch the water heater elements or the pipes, as this can cause damage and reduce the effectiveness of the insulation.

#### Energy Star Standards for Water Heater Insulation

The Energy Star program also sets standards for water heater insulation. For tank insulation, the program requires a minimum R-value of 24 for electric water heaters and 26 for gas water heaters. For pipe insulation, the program requires a minimum R-value of 4 for electric water heaters and 6 for gas water heaters. These standards help to ensure that water heaters are properly insulated and energy-efficient.

In conclusion, water heater insulation is an important aspect of energy efficiency in water heating systems. It can provide significant energy savings and is a cost-effective way to improve the overall energy efficiency of a building. By following the Energy Star standards and using the appropriate insulation materials and techniques, water heaters can be made more energy-efficient and contribute to a more sustainable future.





### Subsection: 19.3a Solar Water Heating Systems

Solar water heating systems are a type of active solar heating system that uses the sun's energy to heat water for domestic or industrial use. These systems are becoming increasingly popular due to their potential to reduce energy costs and their environmental benefits.

#### Components of Solar Water Heating Systems

Solar water heating systems consist of three main components: a solar collector, a storage tank, and a circulation system. The solar collector is the heart of the system. It is responsible for capturing the sun's energy and converting it into heat. The storage tank, often referred to as a hot water cylinder, is where the heated water is stored. The circulation system is responsible for moving the water between the collector and the storage tank.

#### Types of Solar Water Heating Systems

There are two main types of solar water heating systems: direct and indirect. In direct systems, the water is heated directly by the sun in the collector. In indirect systems, a heat transfer fluid, such as glycol, is used to carry the heat from the collector to the storage tank. This allows for better control of the system and can improve efficiency in colder climates.

#### Benefits of Solar Water Heating Systems

Solar water heating systems offer several benefits. They can reduce energy costs by using free solar energy to heat water. They can also reduce greenhouse gas emissions, as they use less fossil fuel-based energy. Additionally, they can provide a reliable and consistent supply of hot water, even on cloudy days.

#### Installation of Solar Water Heating Systems

Installing a solar water heating system requires careful planning and consideration. The system should be installed in a location that receives maximum sunlight, such as a south-facing roof or ground-level area. The collector should be angled at the appropriate angle to catch the maximum amount of sunlight. The storage tank should be insulated to reduce heat loss. The circulation system should be designed to ensure efficient movement of water between the collector and the storage tank.

#### Future Developments in Solar Water Heating Systems

Researchers are constantly working to improve the efficiency and reliability of solar water heating systems. One promising development is the use of phase change materials in the storage tank. These materials can store and release heat at a consistent temperature, improving the efficiency of the system. Additionally, advancements in technology are making solar water heating systems more affordable and accessible to a wider range of consumers.

### Conclusion

Solar water heating systems are a promising technology for reducing energy costs and environmental impact. With careful planning and consideration, they can provide a reliable and consistent supply of hot water for domestic and industrial use. As technology continues to advance, these systems will become even more efficient and accessible, making them a valuable tool in our efforts to reduce our reliance on fossil fuels.





### Subsection: 19.3b Solar Collector Types

Solar collectors are a crucial component of solar water heating systems. They are responsible for capturing the sun's energy and converting it into heat. There are several types of solar collectors, each with its own advantages and disadvantages.

#### Flat Plate Collectors

Flat plate collectors are the most common type of solar collector. They consist of two horizontal pipes at the top and bottom, with a glass cover on top. The pipes are filled with a heat transfer fluid, such as water or glycol, which is heated by the sun. The heat transfer fluid then flows to the storage tank, where it heats the water. Flat plate collectors are relatively simple and inexpensive to manufacture, making them a popular choice for many solar water heating systems.

#### Evacuated Tube Collectors

Evacuated tube collectors are another common type of solar collector. They consist of a series of glass tubes, each containing a heat transfer fluid. The tubes are evacuated, meaning they are sealed and have no air inside. This allows for better heat transfer and efficiency. Evacuated tube collectors are more expensive than flat plate collectors, but they are also more efficient, especially in colder climates.

#### Parabolic Collectors

Parabolic collectors are less common but can be highly efficient. They consist of a parabolic mirror that focuses sunlight onto a small area, heating a heat transfer fluid. Parabolic collectors are more expensive and complex to manufacture, but they can achieve higher temperatures and are more efficient than flat plate and evacuated tube collectors.

#### Comparison of Solar Collector Types

Each type of solar collector has its own advantages and disadvantages. Flat plate collectors are relatively simple and inexpensive, but they may not be as efficient as other types. Evacuated tube collectors are more efficient, but they are also more expensive. Parabolic collectors are the most efficient, but they are also the most expensive and complex to manufacture. The choice of solar collector type will depend on the specific needs and budget of the solar water heating system.





### Subsection: 19.3c Solar Water Heating System Components

Solar water heating systems consist of several components that work together to provide hot water for a building. These components include the solar collectors, heat transfer fluid, storage tank, and control system.

#### Solar Collectors

As discussed in the previous section, solar collectors are the heart of any solar water heating system. They are responsible for capturing the sun's energy and converting it into heat. The type of solar collector used will depend on the specific needs and budget of the system.

#### Heat Transfer Fluid

The heat transfer fluid is the medium that carries heat from the solar collectors to the storage tank. This fluid can be water, glycol, or a mixture of the two. The choice of fluid will depend on the type of solar collector used and the climate.

#### Storage Tank

The storage tank is where the heated water is stored until it is needed. The size and type of storage tank will depend on the size of the building and the amount of hot water needed. The tank should be insulated to prevent heat loss.

#### Control System

The control system is responsible for managing the operation of the solar water heating system. It includes sensors to measure the temperature of the water and the weather, as well as valves and pumps to control the flow of water. The control system may also include a backup heating source, such as a gas or electric water heater, for use on cloudy days or during periods of high demand.

#### Comparison of Solar Water Heating System Components

Each component of a solar water heating system plays a crucial role in the overall performance of the system. The choice of components will depend on the specific needs and budget of the system. It is important to carefully consider the type of solar collector, heat transfer fluid, storage tank, and control system to ensure the most efficient and effective operation of the system.




#### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have explored the fundamentals of water heating systems in buildings. We have discussed the different types of water heating systems, including storage water heaters, instantaneous water heaters, and heat pump water heaters. We have also examined the factors that influence the selection and design of these systems, such as energy efficiency, cost, and environmental impact.

One of the key takeaways from this chapter is the importance of energy efficiency in water heating systems. With the increasing demand for energy and the need to reduce carbon emissions, it is crucial for building owners and designers to consider the energy efficiency of their water heating systems. This can be achieved through the use of advanced technologies, such as heat pump water heaters, which can provide significant energy savings compared to traditional systems.

Another important aspect to consider is the environmental impact of water heating systems. As we have seen, the production and disposal of water heaters can have a significant impact on the environment. Therefore, it is essential to choose systems that are environmentally friendly and have a minimal impact on the planet.

In conclusion, water heating systems play a crucial role in providing hot water for various purposes in buildings. As technology continues to advance, it is important for building owners and designers to stay updated on the latest developments and make informed decisions when it comes to selecting and designing water heating systems.

# Fundamentals of Energy in Buildings: A Comprehensive Guide

## Chapter 20: Energy Recovery Ventilation




### Introduction

Welcome to Chapter 20 of "Fundamentals of Energy in Buildings: A Comprehensive Guide". In this chapter, we will be discussing Building Energy Codes and Standards. These codes and standards play a crucial role in the design and construction of buildings, as they set the minimum requirements for energy efficiency and performance.

Building energy codes and standards are constantly evolving, with new versions being released regularly to keep up with advancements in technology and research. These codes and standards are developed by organizations such as the International Code Council (ICC) and the American Society of Heating, Refrigerating and Air-Conditioning Engineers (ASHRAE).

In this chapter, we will explore the history and development of building energy codes and standards, as well as their impact on the design and construction of buildings. We will also discuss the different types of codes and standards, such as prescriptive and performance-based, and how they are used in the industry.

Furthermore, we will delve into the specifics of these codes and standards, including their requirements for energy efficiency, renewable energy, and occupant comfort. We will also examine the benefits and challenges of implementing these codes and standards, and how they can be effectively integrated into the design and construction process.

By the end of this chapter, you will have a comprehensive understanding of building energy codes and standards and their role in creating sustainable and efficient buildings. So let's dive in and explore the world of building energy codes and standards.




### Subsection: 20.1a International Energy Conservation Code

The International Energy Conservation Code (IECC) is a model energy code developed by the International Code Council (ICC). It is widely adopted in the United States and is used as a base code for many jurisdictional building codes. The IECC is updated every three years, with the latest version being the 2021 IECC.

The IECC is a prescriptive code, meaning it sets specific requirements for energy efficiency in buildings. These requirements are based on climate zone and building type, with more stringent requirements for colder and hotter climates and for larger buildings. The code also includes provisions for renewable energy sources, such as solar and geothermal systems, to encourage the use of sustainable energy sources.

One of the key features of the IECC is its emphasis on building envelope performance. The code requires a certain level of insulation and air tightness, as well as the use of high-performance windows and doors. This helps to reduce heat loss and gain, and therefore, energy consumption.

The IECC also includes provisions for lighting and equipment energy efficiency. For example, it requires the use of energy-efficient lighting fixtures and equipment, as well as the implementation of demand response measures. Demand response is a method of controlling energy use in response to changes in energy demand or pricing.

In addition to these requirements, the IECC also includes provisions for commissioning and testing. Commissioning is the process of verifying that the building meets the energy performance requirements set by the code. This includes testing for air leakage, insulation levels, and equipment efficiency.

The IECC is constantly evolving, with the latest version incorporating new technologies and research findings. It also includes provisions for compliance pathways, allowing for alternative methods of meeting the energy efficiency requirements. These pathways can include performance-based measures, such as energy modeling, or the use of renewable energy systems.

In conclusion, the International Energy Conservation Code is a crucial component of building energy codes and standards. It sets the minimum requirements for energy efficiency in buildings and encourages the use of sustainable energy sources. As technology and research continue to advance, the IECC will continue to evolve and play a vital role in creating sustainable and efficient buildings.





### Subsection: 20.1b ASHRAE 90.1

ASHRAE 90.1 is another widely adopted building energy code in the United States. It is developed by the American Society of Heating, Refrigerating and Air-Conditioning Engineers (ASHRAE) and is updated every three years, with the latest version being the 2019 ASHRAE 90.1.

ASHRAE 90.1 is a performance-based code, meaning it sets energy performance requirements for buildings based on their size and type. These requirements are based on the concept of energy cost budget, where the total energy cost of a building is compared to a baseline building with similar characteristics. The code also includes provisions for renewable energy sources, such as solar and geothermal systems, to encourage the use of sustainable energy sources.

One of the key features of ASHRAE 90.1 is its emphasis on building envelope performance. The code requires a certain level of insulation and air tightness, as well as the use of high-performance windows and doors. This helps to reduce heat loss and gain, and therefore, energy consumption.

The ASHRAE 90.1 also includes provisions for lighting and equipment energy efficiency. For example, it requires the use of energy-efficient lighting fixtures and equipment, as well as the implementation of demand response measures. Demand response is a method of controlling energy use in response to changes in energy demand or pricing.

In addition to these requirements, ASHRAE 90.1 also includes provisions for commissioning and testing. Commissioning is the process of verifying that the building meets the energy performance requirements set by the code. This includes testing for air leakage, insulation levels, and equipment efficiency.

The ASHRAE 90.1 is constantly evolving, with the latest version incorporating new technologies and research findings. It also includes provisions for compliance pathways, allowing for alternative methods of meeting the energy efficiency requirements. These pathways can include the use of advanced energy modeling software, such as DOE-2.1, to simulate and analyze the energy performance of a building.

### Subsection: 20.1c Energy Codes and Standards in Other Countries

While ASHRAE 90.1 and the IECC are widely adopted in the United States, there are also energy codes and standards in other countries that are worth mentioning. These codes and standards are constantly evolving and adapting to the specific needs and concerns of each country.

In Canada, the National Energy Code for Buildings (NECB) is the primary energy code used for new buildings. It is based on the ASHRAE 90.1 and is updated every three years. The NECB also includes provisions for renewable energy sources and demand response measures.

In the European Union, the Energy Performance of Buildings Directive (EPBD) is the primary energy code used for new buildings. It sets energy performance requirements for buildings based on their size and type, and also encourages the use of renewable energy sources. The EPBD is constantly evolving, with the latest version incorporating new technologies and research findings.

In Australia, the National Construction Code (NCC) is the primary energy code used for new buildings. It is based on the ASHRAE 90.1 and is updated every three years. The NCC also includes provisions for renewable energy sources and demand response measures.

These energy codes and standards play a crucial role in promoting energy efficiency and sustainability in buildings around the world. As technology and research continue to advance, these codes and standards will continue to evolve and adapt to meet the changing needs of the built environment.


## Chapter 20: Building Energy Codes and Standards:




### Subsection: 20.1c Local and State Energy Codes

In addition to the national building energy codes, many local and state governments have their own energy codes and standards. These codes are often more stringent than the national codes, reflecting the specific concerns and priorities of the local area.

#### 20.1c.1 Local Energy Codes

Local energy codes are typically developed and enforced by municipalities or counties. These codes may be based on the national codes, but they often include additional requirements or restrictions. For example, a local code may require a higher level of insulation than the national code, or it may prohibit certain types of energy-intensive equipment.

Local energy codes are often driven by the specific needs and characteristics of the local area. For instance, a municipality located in a region with high energy costs may implement a more stringent energy code to encourage energy efficiency and reduce energy costs for building occupants. Similarly, a municipality located in a region with a high risk of natural disasters may include provisions in the energy code to ensure the resilience of buildings during these events.

#### 20.1c.2 State Energy Codes

State energy codes are developed and enforced by state governments. These codes are often more comprehensive than local codes, covering a wider range of building types and energy systems. State energy codes are typically based on the national codes, but they may include additional requirements or restrictions.

State energy codes are often driven by state-level energy policies and goals. For example, a state with a goal of reducing its overall energy consumption may implement a state energy code that requires more stringent energy efficiency measures in buildings. Similarly, a state with a high reliance on renewable energy sources may include provisions in the energy code to encourage the use of these sources in buildings.

#### 20.1c.3 Compliance with Local and State Energy Codes

Compliance with local and state energy codes is typically enforced through building permits and inspections. Building owners must obtain a permit before beginning construction, and the building must meet all the requirements of the local and state energy codes to obtain the permit. The building is then inspected at various stages of construction to ensure compliance with the codes.

In some cases, building owners may be able to obtain a variance from the energy code requirements. This is typically granted when the building owner can demonstrate that meeting the code requirements would be impractical or excessively costly. However, variances are typically limited in scope and must be approved by the local or state authority having jurisdiction.




#### 20.2a LEED

The Leadership in Energy and Environmental Design (LEED) rating system is a globally recognized symbol of sustainability achievement. It provides a framework for developing sustainable buildings, homes, and communities. The LEED rating system is administered by the U.S. Green Building Council (USGBC), a non-profit organization committed to transforming the way buildings and communities are designed, constructed, and operated.

#### 20.2a.1 LEED for Buildings

LEED for Buildings is the most widely used green building rating system in the world. It provides a framework for developing sustainable buildings that are environmentally responsible and resource-efficient. The system is based on five key categories: sustainable site, energy and atmosphere, water efficiency, materials and resources, and indoor environmental quality. Each category has specific criteria that must be met to achieve LEED certification.

##### Sustainable Site

The sustainable site category focuses on the location and design of the building. It encourages the use of existing buildings, reducing the need for new construction materials and energy. It also promotes the use of public transportation and alternative modes of transportation, reducing the environmental impact of vehicle trips. The category also includes criteria for the protection of existing vegetation and the use of native plants, which can help preserve natural habitats and reduce the need for irrigation.

##### Energy and Atmosphere

The energy and atmosphere category focuses on the energy efficiency of the building. It encourages the use of renewable energy sources and the implementation of energy-efficient technologies. The category also includes criteria for the commissioning of building systems, which ensures that the systems are installed and calibrated correctly. This can help reduce energy waste and improve the overall performance of the building.

##### Water Efficiency

The water efficiency category focuses on the efficient use of water in the building. It encourages the use of low-flow fixtures and high-efficiency toilets, which can reduce water consumption by up to 60%. The category also includes criteria for the use of rainwater and greywater, which can help reduce the demand for potable water.

##### Materials and Resources

The materials and resources category focuses on the use of sustainable materials in the building. It encourages the use of recycled and renewable materials, which can help reduce the environmental impact of construction. The category also includes criteria for the use of materials with a high recycled content, which can help reduce waste and promote circularity.

##### Indoor Environmental Quality

The indoor environmental quality category focuses on the health and well-being of building occupants. It encourages the use of low-emitting materials and the implementation of strategies to improve indoor air quality. The category also includes criteria for the use of natural light and views, which can help improve the quality of the indoor environment.

#### 20.2a.2 LEED for Homes

LEED for Homes is a rating system specifically designed for residential buildings. It provides a framework for developing sustainable homes that are environmentally responsible and resource-efficient. The system is based on five key categories: location and linkages, sustainable site, energy and atmosphere, water efficiency, and materials and resources. Each category has specific criteria that must be met to achieve LEED certification.

##### Location and Linkages

The location and linkages category focuses on the location of the home and its connection to the surrounding community. It encourages the use of existing homes, reducing the need for new construction materials and energy. It also promotes the use of public transportation and alternative modes of transportation, reducing the environmental impact of vehicle trips. The category also includes criteria for the protection of existing vegetation and the use of native plants, which can help preserve natural habitats and reduce the need for irrigation.

##### Sustainable Site

The sustainable site category focuses on the design of the home and its site. It encourages the use of existing homes, reducing the need for new construction materials and energy. It also promotes the use of public transportation and alternative modes of transportation, reducing the environmental impact of vehicle trips. The category also includes criteria for the protection of existing vegetation and the use of native plants, which can help preserve natural habitats and reduce the need for irrigation.

##### Energy and Atmosphere

The energy and atmosphere category focuses on the energy efficiency of the home. It encourages the use of renewable energy sources and the implementation of energy-efficient technologies. The category also includes criteria for the commissioning of building systems, which ensures that the systems are installed and calibrated correctly. This can help reduce energy waste and improve the overall performance of the home.

##### Water Efficiency

The water efficiency category focuses on the efficient use of water in the home. It encourages the use of low-flow fixtures and high-efficiency toilets, which can reduce water consumption by up to 60%. The category also includes criteria for the use of rainwater and greywater, which can help reduce the demand for potable water.

##### Materials and Resources

The materials and resources category focuses on the use of sustainable materials in the home. It encourages the use of recycled and renewable materials, which can help reduce the environmental impact of construction. The category also includes criteria for the use of materials with a high recycled content, which can help reduce waste and promote circularity.

#### 20.2a.3 LEED for Communities

LEED for Communities is a rating system that evaluates the sustainability of a community as a whole. It is designed to guide the development of sustainable communities that meet the needs of their residents, are environmentally responsible, and are economically viable. The system is based on five key categories: location and linkages, neighborhood design, green buildings, innovation and design process, and regional and community benefits. Each category has specific criteria that must be met to achieve LEED certification.

##### Location and Linkages

The location and linkages category focuses on the location of the community and its connection to the surrounding area. It encourages the development of communities in locations that are already built up, reducing the need for new construction materials and energy. It also promotes the use of public transportation and alternative modes of transportation, reducing the environmental impact of vehicle trips. The category also includes criteria for the protection of existing vegetation and the use of native plants, which can help preserve natural habitats and reduce the need for irrigation.

##### Neighborhood Design

The neighborhood design category focuses on the design of the community and its buildings. It encourages the use of existing buildings, reducing the need for new construction materials and energy. It also promotes the use of public transportation and alternative modes of transportation, reducing the environmental impact of vehicle trips. The category also includes criteria for the protection of existing vegetation and the use of native plants, which can help preserve natural habitats and reduce the need for irrigation.

##### Green Buildings

The green buildings category focuses on the energy and water efficiency of the buildings in the community. It encourages the use of renewable energy sources and the implementation of energy-efficient technologies. The category also includes criteria for the use of sustainable materials and the implementation of strategies to reduce waste.

##### Innovation and Design Process

The innovation and design process category focuses on the innovative and sustainable practices used in the development of the community. It encourages the use of cutting-edge technologies and strategies to reduce the environmental impact of the community. The category also includes criteria for the involvement of the community in the design process, promoting a sense of ownership and responsibility for the sustainability of the community.

##### Regional and Community Benefits

The regional and community benefits category focuses on the positive impact of the community on the surrounding area. It encourages the development of communities that benefit the regional and local economy, environment, and society. The category also includes criteria for the preservation of open space and the promotion of community health and well-being.

#### 20.2b Passive House

Passive House is a building standard that aims to achieve a high level of energy efficiency. It was developed in the 1990s by the Physikalisch-Technische Bundesanstalt (PTB) in Germany and has since been adopted in many countries around the world. The standard is based on the principles of passive design, which focuses on maximizing the use of natural resources and minimizing energy consumption.

##### Passive House Standard

The Passive House standard is based on four key principles:

1. Thermal comfort: The building must provide a comfortable indoor environment for its occupants without the need for active heating or cooling systems.
2. Energy efficiency: The building must have a very high level of energy efficiency, with a primary energy demand of no more than 120 kWh/m²a.
3. Airtightness: The building must have an airtight envelope to minimize heat loss and gain.
4. Ventilation: The building must have a mechanical ventilation system that provides a constant supply of fresh air while recovering heat from exhaust air.

##### Passive House Design

Passive House design is a holistic approach that considers all aspects of the building, from its orientation and layout to its materials and systems. The goal is to create a building that is comfortable, healthy, and sustainable.

###### Orientation and Layout

The orientation and layout of the building play a crucial role in achieving passive design. The building should be oriented to take advantage of natural light and heat from the sun, while minimizing exposure to direct sunlight. The layout should also be designed to maximize the use of natural ventilation and minimize the need for artificial lighting and heating.

###### Materials and Systems

The materials and systems used in a Passive House must be carefully selected to meet the high energy efficiency standards. This includes the use of high-performance insulation, triple-pane windows, and airtight construction. The building must also have a mechanical ventilation system with heat recovery to provide a constant supply of fresh air while recovering heat from exhaust air.

###### Energy Modeling

Energy modeling is an essential part of Passive House design. It involves using computer software to simulate the energy performance of the building and make adjustments to the design to meet the Passive House standards. This process helps to ensure that the building will meet the energy efficiency requirements and provides valuable information for the construction and operation of the building.

##### Passive House Certification

Passive House certification is available for both buildings and components. The Passive House Institute (PHI) in Germany is responsible for certifying Passive House buildings and components, and there are also national certification schemes in some countries. The certification process involves a rigorous quality assurance process, including on-site inspections and energy calculations.

##### Passive House in Practice

Passive House design has been successfully applied in a variety of building types, including single-family homes, multi-family buildings, and commercial buildings. The principles of Passive House can also be applied to retrofits, making it a valuable tool for improving the energy efficiency of existing buildings.

In conclusion, Passive House is a highly energy-efficient building standard that is based on the principles of passive design. It requires careful consideration of all aspects of the building, from its orientation and layout to its materials and systems. With its focus on energy efficiency, comfort, and sustainability, Passive House is a valuable tool for creating a better built environment.

#### 20.2c WELL Building Standard

The WELL Building Standard (WELL) is a performance-based certification system for buildings, interior spaces, and communities. It is designed to improve the health and well-being of building occupants by addressing seven categories of features: Air, Water, Nourishment, Light, Fitness, Comfort, and Mind. Each category has a set of features that must be met to achieve certification.

##### WELL Building Standard Features

The WELL Building Standard features are designed to enhance the health and well-being of building occupants. They are divided into two types: mandatory and optimizing.

###### Mandatory Features

Mandatory features are essential for achieving WELL certification. They include:

1. Air: The building must provide a healthy indoor air quality, with a focus on minimizing exposure to pollutants and promoting fresh air.
2. Water: The building must provide access to clean and safe drinking water, with a focus on reducing exposure to contaminants.
3. Nourishment: The building must provide access to healthy food options, with a focus on promoting nutrient-rich diets.
4. Light: The building must provide access to natural light and views, with a focus on promoting circadian rhythm and reducing exposure to artificial light.
5. Fitness: The building must provide opportunities for physical activity, with a focus on promoting a sedentary lifestyle.
6. Comfort: The building must provide a comfortable and safe environment, with a focus on minimizing exposure to noise and other stressors.
7. Mind: The building must provide opportunities for mental well-being, with a focus on promoting stress management and cognitive function.

###### Optimizing Features

Optimizing features are not mandatory for certification, but they can enhance the health and well-being of building occupants. They include:

1. Air: The building must provide a high level of indoor air quality, with a focus on minimizing exposure to pollutants and promoting fresh air.
2. Water: The building must provide access to clean and safe drinking water, with a focus on reducing exposure to contaminants and promoting hydration.
3. Nourishment: The building must provide access to healthy food options, with a focus on promoting nutrient-rich diets and reducing exposure to unhealthy food options.
4. Light: The building must provide access to natural light and views, with a focus on promoting circadian rhythm and reducing exposure to artificial light.
5. Fitness: The building must provide opportunities for physical activity, with a focus on promoting a sedentary lifestyle and reducing exposure to sedentary behaviors.
6. Comfort: The building must provide a comfortable and safe environment, with a focus on minimizing exposure to noise and other stressors and promoting a sense of safety and security.
7. Mind: The building must provide opportunities for mental well-being, with a focus on promoting stress management and cognitive function and reducing exposure to stress and cognitive impairment.

##### WELL Building Standard Certification

WELL certification is available for both new construction and existing buildings. The certification process involves a rigorous quality assurance process, including on-site inspections and energy calculations. The certification is valid for three years and can be renewed by demonstrating ongoing compliance with the WELL features.

##### WELL Building Standard and Passive House

While the WELL Building Standard and Passive House have different focuses and approaches, there are some commonalities between the two. Both standards aim to improve the health and well-being of building occupants, and both emphasize the importance of indoor air quality, natural light, and physical activity. However, the WELL Building Standard also addresses aspects such as nourishment, comfort, and mind, which are not specifically addressed in the Passive House standard.

### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have explored the various building energy codes and standards that are in place to ensure the efficient use of energy in buildings. These codes and standards are constantly evolving as technology advances and our understanding of energy use improves. It is important for architects, engineers, and builders to stay up-to-date with these codes and standards to ensure that their designs and constructions meet the necessary energy efficiency requirements.

We have also discussed the importance of considering energy use in the design phase of a building, rather than just focusing on it during the construction phase. By incorporating energy efficiency into the design, we can create buildings that are not only comfortable and functional, but also sustainable and environmentally friendly.

In conclusion, building energy codes and standards play a crucial role in promoting energy efficiency in the construction industry. As technology continues to advance, so will these codes and standards, pushing the industry towards a more sustainable future.

## Chapter: Chapter 21: Energy Storage

### Introduction

Energy storage is a critical component in the broader context of energy systems. It is the process of capturing and storing energy for later use. This chapter, "Energy Storage," will delve into the fundamental principles and applications of energy storage in the context of buildings.

Energy storage plays a pivotal role in the energy efficiency of buildings. It allows for the management of energy demand and supply, enabling the use of renewable energy sources and reducing the reliance on traditional energy sources. The storage of energy can be in various forms, such as thermal, electrical, or chemical, each with its unique characteristics and applications.

In the realm of building energy systems, energy storage can be used to store excess energy generated during peak production periods, which can then be used during periods of low production. This helps in balancing the energy demand and supply, thereby improving the overall energy efficiency of the building.

This chapter will also explore the various types of energy storage technologies, their advantages and disadvantages, and their applications in building energy systems. It will also discuss the challenges and opportunities associated with energy storage in the context of buildings.

The chapter will also touch upon the role of energy storage in the integration of renewable energy sources into building energy systems. With the increasing focus on sustainable energy solutions, energy storage is becoming an integral part of building design and operation.

In conclusion, this chapter aims to provide a comprehensive understanding of energy storage in the context of building energy systems. It will equip readers with the knowledge and tools to understand and apply energy storage in their own building energy systems.




#### 20.2b Energy Star for Buildings

Energy Star for Buildings is a voluntary program that helps building owners and managers improve the energy efficiency of their buildings. It is a part of the larger Energy Star program, which was established by the U.S. Environmental Protection Agency (EPA) in 1992. The Energy Star program aims to reduce greenhouse gas emissions and promote energy efficiency in various sectors, including buildings.

##### Energy Star for Buildings: Overview

The Energy Star for Buildings program provides a framework for building owners and managers to measure and improve the energy performance of their buildings. It is based on the concept of benchmarking, which involves comparing a building's energy consumption to that of similar buildings. This allows building owners and managers to identify areas for improvement and track progress over time.

The program uses a rating system that ranges from 1 to 100, with 100 being the most energy-efficient. A building must achieve a rating of 75 or above to be eligible for the Energy Star certification. The rating is calculated based on the building's energy consumption, square footage, and weekly operating hours.

##### Energy Star for Buildings: Benefits

Participating in the Energy Star for Buildings program can provide several benefits to building owners and managers. These include:

- Cost savings: By improving energy efficiency, building owners and managers can reduce their energy costs and save money.
- Improved occupant comfort: Energy-efficient buildings often have better indoor air quality and temperature control, leading to improved occupant comfort.
- Enhanced reputation: Building owners and managers can use the Energy Star certification as a marketing tool to attract tenants and customers.
- Compliance with regulations: Many states and cities have regulations in place to reduce greenhouse gas emissions. Participating in the Energy Star program can help building owners and managers comply with these regulations.

##### Energy Star for Buildings: Participation

Participation in the Energy Star for Buildings program is voluntary. Building owners and managers can choose to participate in the program at any time. However, it is important to note that the program is most effective when implemented as part of a comprehensive energy management plan. This includes setting energy performance goals, implementing energy-efficient technologies, and conducting regular energy audits.

In conclusion, the Energy Star for Buildings program is a valuable tool for building owners and managers to improve the energy efficiency of their buildings. By participating in the program, building owners and managers can save money, improve occupant comfort, and contribute to reducing greenhouse gas emissions. 


#### 20.2c Passive House

Passive House is a building standard that aims to achieve a high level of energy efficiency. It was developed in the 1990s by physicist Wolfgang Feist and is now administered by the Passive House Institute in Germany. The standard is based on the principles of passive design, which involves using natural resources and techniques to reduce energy consumption.

##### Passive House: Overview

The Passive House standard is based on five key principles:

1. Thermal comfort: The building must provide a comfortable indoor environment for its occupants without the need for excessive heating or cooling.
2. Energy efficiency: The building must have a very high level of energy efficiency, with a primary energy demand of no more than 120 kWh/m²a (40 kWh/ft²a).
3. Airtightness: The building must have an airtight envelope to prevent heat loss and gain.
4. Ventilation: The building must have a mechanical ventilation system that provides fresh air while recovering heat from exhaust air.
5. Renewable energy: The building must use renewable energy sources for heating and cooling, with a maximum of 10% of the total energy demand coming from non-renewable sources.

##### Passive House: Benefits

Participating in the Passive House standard can provide several benefits to building owners and occupants. These include:

- Extremely low heating and cooling demand: The Passive House standard aims to achieve a primary energy demand of no more than 120 kWh/m²a (40 kWh/ft²a). This results in extremely low heating and cooling demand, leading to significant cost savings.
- Comfortable indoor environment: The focus on thermal comfort ensures that occupants are able to maintain a comfortable indoor temperature without the need for excessive heating or cooling.
- Healthy indoor air quality: The emphasis on airtightness and mechanical ventilation with heat recovery helps to maintain healthy indoor air quality.
- Reduced environmental impact: By using renewable energy sources and achieving a very high level of energy efficiency, Passive House buildings have a reduced environmental impact compared to traditional buildings.

##### Passive House: Certification

The Passive House Institute offers certification for buildings that meet the Passive House standard. This certification is based on a performance-based approach, where buildings are evaluated based on their actual energy consumption. The certification process involves a detailed energy calculation, known as the Passive House Planning Package (PHPP), which takes into account the building's design, location, and occupant behavior.

In addition to the certification, the Passive House Institute also offers training and education programs for professionals in the building industry. These programs aim to promote the principles and techniques of passive design and help to increase the number of Passive House buildings around the world.





#### 20.2c Passive House

Passive House is a building standard that aims to achieve a high level of energy efficiency. It was developed in the 1990s in Germany and has since been adopted in many countries around the world. The standard is based on the principles of passive design, which involves using natural resources such as sunlight and ventilation to reduce the need for heating and cooling.

##### Passive House: Overview

The Passive House standard is based on five key principles:

1. Thermal comfort: The building must provide a comfortable indoor environment for its occupants.
2. Energy efficiency: The building must have a very low energy demand for heating and cooling.
3. Airtightness: The building must have a very low air permeability to minimize heat loss.
4. Ventilation: The building must have a controlled ventilation system to provide fresh air and remove stale air.
5. Windows: The building must have high-quality windows that provide natural light and heat gain.

To achieve these principles, Passive House buildings are designed to have a very high level of insulation, airtight construction, and efficient ventilation systems. This results in a building that requires very little energy for heating and cooling, making it highly energy-efficient.

##### Passive House: Benefits

There are several benefits to building to the Passive House standard. These include:

- Energy savings: Passive House buildings can achieve energy savings of up to 90% compared to typical buildings.
- Comfort: The high level of insulation and ventilation provides a comfortable indoor environment for occupants.
- Health: The controlled ventilation system helps to maintain good indoor air quality, reducing the risk of health issues.
- Durability: The high-quality construction and materials used in Passive House buildings can result in longer lifespans.
- Cost savings: While the initial cost of building to the Passive House standard may be higher, the long-term energy savings can result in significant cost savings.

##### Passive House: Applications

The Passive House standard can be applied to a wide range of building types, from single-family homes to large commercial buildings. It is particularly well-suited to buildings in colder climates, where heating is a major energy consumption. However, with the right design and construction, Passive House principles can also be applied in warmer climates to achieve energy efficiency.

In the next section, we will explore another energy standard for buildings: Energy Star for Buildings.




#### 20.3a Prescriptive Compliance

Prescriptive compliance is a method of ensuring that building energy codes and standards are met. It involves following specific guidelines and regulations set forth by the code or standard, rather than relying on performance-based compliance. This method is often used in conjunction with performance-based compliance to provide a more comprehensive approach to energy efficiency in buildings.

##### Prescriptive Compliance: Overview

Prescriptive compliance is based on the principle of "do as I say, not as I do." It involves following a set of rules and regulations that are specified in the code or standard. These rules may include specific design requirements, such as insulation levels, or specific construction techniques, such as air sealing. The goal of prescriptive compliance is to ensure that buildings are designed and constructed in a way that meets the energy efficiency requirements set forth by the code or standard.

##### Prescriptive Compliance: Benefits

There are several benefits to using prescriptive compliance in building energy codes and standards. These include:

- Simplicity: Prescriptive compliance is often easier to understand and implement than performance-based compliance. It provides clear guidelines and requirements, making it easier for designers and builders to ensure compliance.
- Consistency: Prescriptive compliance ensures consistency in energy efficiency requirements across different types of buildings. This can help to level the playing field and prevent unfair competition between different types of buildings.
- Verification: Prescriptive compliance allows for easy verification of compliance. By following specific guidelines and regulations, it is easier to demonstrate that a building meets the energy efficiency requirements set forth by the code or standard.

##### Prescriptive Compliance: Limitations

While prescriptive compliance has many benefits, it also has some limitations. These include:

- Lack of flexibility: Prescriptive compliance can be limiting in terms of design and construction options. It may not allow for the use of innovative technologies or techniques that could potentially improve energy efficiency.
- Potential for over-compliance: In some cases, prescriptive compliance may result in buildings being designed and constructed to a higher level of energy efficiency than is required by the code or standard. This can lead to unnecessary costs and resources being spent.
- Potential for under-compliance: On the other hand, prescriptive compliance may also result in buildings being designed and constructed to a lower level of energy efficiency than is required by the code or standard. This can lead to buildings not meeting the intended energy efficiency goals.

In conclusion, prescriptive compliance is an important aspect of building energy codes and standards. It provides a clear and consistent approach to energy efficiency, but it also has some limitations that must be considered. By using a combination of prescriptive and performance-based compliance, buildings can achieve the highest level of energy efficiency while also allowing for flexibility and innovation in design and construction.





#### 20.3b Performance Compliance

Performance compliance is another method of ensuring that building energy codes and standards are met. It involves evaluating the performance of a building based on its energy efficiency, rather than following specific guidelines and regulations. This method is often used in conjunction with prescriptive compliance to provide a more comprehensive approach to energy efficiency in buildings.

##### Performance Compliance: Overview

Performance compliance is based on the principle of "show me, don't tell me." It involves evaluating the actual energy performance of a building, rather than following a set of rules and regulations. This method allows for more flexibility and innovation in building design, as it does not restrict designers to specific design requirements or construction techniques. The goal of performance compliance is to ensure that buildings are designed and constructed in a way that meets the energy efficiency requirements set forth by the code or standard.

##### Performance Compliance: Benefits

There are several benefits to using performance compliance in building energy codes and standards. These include:

- Flexibility: Performance compliance allows for more flexibility in building design, as it does not restrict designers to specific design requirements or construction techniques. This can lead to more innovative and efficient building designs.
- Adaptability: Performance compliance allows for buildings to adapt to changing energy efficiency requirements. As technology advances and energy costs change, buildings can be evaluated and modified to meet new standards.
- Verification: Performance compliance allows for easy verification of compliance. By evaluating the actual energy performance of a building, it is easier to demonstrate that a building meets the energy efficiency requirements set forth by the code or standard.

##### Performance Compliance: Limitations

While performance compliance has many benefits, it also has some limitations. These include:

- Complexity: Performance compliance can be more complex and time-consuming than prescriptive compliance. It requires more data collection and analysis, which can be challenging for some building owners and operators.
- Uncertainty: Performance compliance relies on predictions and simulations, which can be uncertain. This can lead to discrepancies between predicted and actual energy performance, making it difficult to ensure compliance.
- Cost: The additional data collection and analysis required for performance compliance can be costly. This can be a barrier for some building owners and operators, especially those with limited resources.

Despite these limitations, performance compliance is becoming increasingly popular in building energy codes and standards. It allows for more flexibility and adaptability, and can lead to more efficient building designs. As technology continues to advance and energy costs continue to rise, performance compliance will likely become the dominant method for ensuring compliance with building energy codes and standards.





#### 20.3c Energy Modeling for Compliance

Energy modeling is a crucial aspect of building energy codes and standards compliance. It involves using computer simulations to predict the energy performance of a building, allowing designers to make informed decisions about energy efficiency. Energy modeling can be used in conjunction with both prescriptive and performance compliance methods.

##### Energy Modeling: Overview

Energy modeling is a powerful tool that allows designers to evaluate the energy performance of a building before it is constructed. This is done by inputting data about the building, such as its size, orientation, and construction materials, into a computer simulation program. The program then calculates the building's energy performance, taking into account factors such as heating and cooling loads, lighting, and appliances. This allows designers to make adjustments to the building design to improve its energy efficiency.

##### Energy Modeling: Benefits

There are several benefits to using energy modeling in building energy codes and standards compliance. These include:

- Predictability: Energy modeling allows designers to predict the energy performance of a building before it is constructed. This allows for more informed decision-making and can help designers identify potential energy efficiency issues early on.
- Cost-effectiveness: By using energy modeling, designers can identify the most cost-effective ways to improve a building's energy efficiency. This can save time and resources in the long run.
- Compliance: Energy modeling can help ensure that a building meets the energy efficiency requirements set forth by building energy codes and standards. This can help designers avoid costly redesigns or non-compliance penalties.

##### Energy Modeling: Limitations

While energy modeling is a powerful tool, it is not without its limitations. These include:

- Simplifications: Energy modeling programs often make simplifications and assumptions about a building's energy performance. This can lead to inaccuracies in the predicted energy performance.
- User error: Energy modeling programs require a certain level of expertise to use effectively. Errors in inputting data or understanding the program's capabilities can lead to inaccurate results.
- Uncertainty: Energy modeling programs rely on assumptions and estimates about a building's energy performance. This can lead to uncertainty in the predicted energy performance.

Despite these limitations, energy modeling is a valuable tool in building energy codes and standards compliance. It allows designers to make informed decisions about energy efficiency and can help ensure that buildings meet the energy efficiency requirements set forth by building energy codes and standards.

In this chapter, we have explored the various building energy codes and standards that are in place to ensure the efficient use of energy in buildings. We have discussed the importance of these codes and standards in promoting sustainable building practices and reducing the environmental impact of buildings. We have also looked at the different types of energy codes and standards, including prescriptive and performance-based codes, and how they are used in different regions.

One of the key takeaways from this chapter is the importance of understanding and adhering to building energy codes and standards. These codes and standards are constantly evolving and it is crucial for building professionals to stay updated with the latest developments. By following these codes and standards, we can ensure that buildings are designed and constructed in a way that is energy-efficient and environmentally friendly.

As we move towards a more sustainable future, it is essential that we continue to improve and enforce building energy codes and standards. This will not only help in reducing the energy consumption of buildings, but also in promoting a healthier and more comfortable living environment for occupants. It is our responsibility as building professionals to play a crucial role in achieving this goal.

One of the key takeaways from this chapter is the importance of understanding and adhering to building energy codes and standards. These codes and standards are constantly evolving and it is crucial for building professionals to stay updated with the latest developments. By following these codes and standards, we can ensure that buildings are designed and constructed in a way that is energy-efficient and environmentally friendly.

As we move towards a more sustainable future, it is essential that we continue to improve and enforce building energy codes and standards. This will not only help in reducing the energy consumption of buildings, but also in promoting a healthier and more comfortable living environment for occupants. It is our responsibility as building professionals to play a crucial role in achieving this goal.

### Exercises
#### Exercise 1
Research and compare the building energy codes and standards in your local area with those in a neighboring area. What are the similarities and differences? How do these differences impact the design and construction of buildings in each area?

#### Exercise 2
Choose a specific building energy code or standard and research its history and development. What were the key factors that led to its creation? How has it evolved over time?

#### Exercise 3
Design a building that meets all the requirements of a specific building energy code or standard. Consider the design decisions that had to be made to achieve compliance.

#### Exercise 4
Investigate the impact of building energy codes and standards on the construction industry. How have these codes and standards changed the way buildings are designed and constructed? What are the benefits and challenges of these changes?

#### Exercise 5
Discuss the role of building energy codes and standards in promoting sustainable building practices. How can these codes and standards be improved to further promote sustainability?


## Chapter: Fundamentals of Energy in Buildings: A Comprehensive Guide

### Introduction

In today's world, energy efficiency has become a crucial aspect in the design and construction of buildings. With the increasing concern for the environment and the depletion of natural resources, it has become essential to find ways to reduce energy consumption in buildings. This is where building energy audits come into play. Building energy audits are systematic processes that evaluate the energy performance of a building, identify areas of energy waste, and suggest ways to improve energy efficiency.

In this chapter, we will delve into the fundamentals of building energy audits. We will explore the various methods and techniques used to evaluate the energy performance of a building. We will also discuss the importance of building energy audits in achieving energy efficiency and reducing the environmental impact of buildings. Additionally, we will cover the different types of building energy audits, including pre-occupancy, post-occupancy, and continuous audits.

Furthermore, we will also touch upon the role of building energy audits in the design and construction process. We will discuss how building energy audits can be used to identify energy-efficient design strategies and inform the selection of energy-efficient building systems. We will also explore the benefits of conducting building energy audits during the design and construction phase, such as cost savings and improved occupant comfort.

Overall, this chapter aims to provide a comprehensive guide to building energy audits, equipping readers with the necessary knowledge and tools to conduct energy audits in their own buildings. By the end of this chapter, readers will have a better understanding of the importance of building energy audits and how they can be used to achieve energy efficiency in buildings.
